He was explicit that most NFT projects would fail, but that the underlying technology would be transformational.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** When evaluating emerging asset classes built on new technology, separate the speculative mania from the structural infrastructure shift. The infrastructure question is almost always more important than the price question.

**Gary launched VeeFriends in 2021 as a direct demonstration of his NFT thesis in practice — each token represented access to Gary himself in some form, embedding real utility into the asset. The project was designed to align his financial incentives with long-term community health rather than short-term speculation. He explicitly positioned it as a 40-year brand-building exercise, not a quick flip.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** The most durable speculative assets are ones where the creator's long-term incentives are structurally aligned with token-holder value. When evaluating NFT or community-based projects, map the incentive architecture before the price chart.

**Gary has been vocal that the NFT crash of 2022 did not change his fundamental view on the technology or the thesis behind VeeFriends. He drew an explicit parallel to the dot-com crash of 2000 — most companies went to zero, but the internet itself became the defining infrastructure of the next two decades. He argued that the speculators exiting NFTs in 2022 were confusing the asset bubble with the technology's trajectory.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** Distinguish between the speculative layer and the infrastructure layer in any emerging technology investment. When the bubble pops, the technology question is often more important than the price question.

**Gary has framed his sports card investments as a direct application of his core attention-arbitrage principle: in 2019 and early 2020, sports cards had massive cultural resonance in certain communities but were almost entirely ignored by mainstream financial media and institutional buyers. That gap between cultural passion and financial legitimacy was exactly the window he has consistently identified as his highest-conviction opportunity zone.** ([source](SUCCESS Comes Once You FIGURE THIS OUT! | Gary Vee | Top 10 Rules))

**Implication:** Map the distance between where passionate communities are spending time and money versus where financial legitimacy has been formally conferred. The gap between those two points is where the best speculative returns live.

**Gary has consistently argued that the collectibles market is being permanently transformed by the demographic handoff — as millennials and Gen Z become the dominant wealth-holding generation, the assets they will bid up are not the ones their parents valued but the ones that defined their own childhoods and cultural identities. Sports cards, sneakers, video games, and eventually digital assets are the natural stores of value for a generation that grew up differently. He has positioned himself to benefit from this structural demographic shift.** ([source](When You Hyper-Focus on the Results You Miss New Opportunities | The Quest Podcast))

**Implication:** Model your long-term speculative positions around generational wealth transfer, not current market conventions. The assets the next dominant wealth-holding cohort will prize are already visible in what they are passionately collecting today.

**Gary has described his angel investing as an extension of his content and relationship strategy — he invested in founders he had genuine relationships with, and those relationships were built through years of giving value, advice, and attention before any transaction occurred. The investments were often the right-hook at the end of a long jab sequence with a founder he had supported for years. The financial return was downstream of the relational return.** ([source](Why Gary Vee Was My First Big Investment: Matt Higgins and Lewis Howes))

**Implication:** The best investment deals don't come from cold outreach or pitch competitions — they come from genuine long-term relationships where you've already given more than you've taken. Build the relationship before you need the deal.

**Gary has argued publicly and repeatedly that sports cards represent a legitimate, serious asset class — not a hobby sideshow. His thesis rests on the intersection of cultural nostalgia, manufactured scarcity, and a new generation of wealthy buyers who grew up loving the sport. He has invested eight figures of his own capital into this bet, treating it with the same analytical rigor he applies to any venture investment.** ([source](Joe Rogan Experience #910 - Gary Vaynerchuk))

**Implication:** Before dismissing any asset class as 'niche' or 'collector stuff,' ask whether the cultural passion behind it is real, whether supply is genuinely constrained, and whether new money is entering the market. Those three factors together are the signal.

**Gary's framework for collectible investing mirrors his framework for attention arbitrage.** find what the mainstream market hasn't yet priced in and move before consensus arrives. He identified the sports card resurgence years before it became a widely covered financial trend, buying when the asset was still considered a nostalgic curiosity rather than a portfolio holding. By the time financial media started covering it, he was already deeply positioned. ([source](Joe Rogan Experience #910 - Gary Vaynerchuk))

**Implication:** The edge in any speculative market is always in the gap between where cultural passion already exists and where institutional capital hasn't yet followed. Study passion communities, not financial reports, to find those gaps first.

**Gary has argued that most financial advisors are systematically blind to collectible and cultural assets because their valuation models cannot account for nostalgia, community passion, or generational transfer of taste. The very things that make sports cards, wine, and art seem speculative to traditional finance — their emotional basis — are precisely what makes their demand durable. Emotion-backed scarcity is a more stable long-term driver than earnings-backed scarcity.** ([source](Joe Rogan Experience #910 - Gary Vaynerchuk))

**Implication:** When the financial establishment cannot model an asset's value, that's often a signal that the value is real but non-quantitative. Train yourself to assess community passion depth alongside balance sheet metrics.

**Gary has explicitly noted that his wine background gave him a template for how passion markets work at scale — a small number of obsessive collectors drive price discovery, then mass affluent buyers follow, then mainstream financial recognition arrives last. He observed this exact sequence in fine wine and used it as a pattern-matching framework when evaluating whether sports cards and NFTs would follow the same trajectory. Every collectible market that achieves legitimacy goes through the same phases.** ([source](Wine Library TV origin; Joe Rogan Experience #910 - Gary Vaynerchuk))

**Implication:** Study the historical price discovery sequence in established passion markets — fine wine, vintage watches, art — and use that sequence as a diagnostic template when evaluating whether emerging collectible categories are at an early, middle, or late stage of the same cycle.

**Gary treats speculative investments with strict emotional detachment once capital is deployed — he does not panic-sell, does not chase short-term price validation, and is comfortable holding positions for a decade or more. He has described his psychological posture toward volatile assets as a direct product of his immigrant-family gratitude framework: because he came from nothing, he is never 'betting the house.' That baseline security allows him to think and hold in timeframes most investors can't access.** ([source](Tony Robbins, Unshakeable, Gratitude & Focusing on Your Steak | #AskGaryVee 242))

**Implication:** Your ability to hold a long-term speculative position is a function of your psychological baseline, not your analytical confidence. Building genuine gratitude for what you already have is, counterintuitively, one of the most practical risk-management strategies available.

**Gary has been publicly skeptical of diversified index investing as a life philosophy for entrepreneurially-minded people, arguing that concentration of attention and capital into things you genuinely understand and believe in outperforms cautious diversification over a lifetime. He is not advising recklessness — he is arguing against the kind of diversification that is really just a hedge against self-knowledge. Knowing what you know is itself a compounding asset.** ([source](Tony Robbins & Gary Vee: Creating a Money Machine | Tony Robbins))

**Implication:** Diversification is the right strategy for people who don't have a genuine edge. If you do have deep cultural or domain insight, broad diversification dilutes it. Bet bigger on what you actually understand.

**Gary built VaynerSports in part as an infrastructure play to be closer to the athlete ecosystem that overlaps with his sports card and collectible investment thesis. He has argued that proximity to the actual cultural production — the athletes, the moments, the stories — gives him information advantages that pure financial investors in the collectibles market simply cannot access. Integration across the value chain is itself an investment strategy.** ([source](Adweek: VaynerMedia Rapid Ascent))

**Implication:** Invest in assets where your existing business activities or relationships give you non-public informational advantages. The edge in speculative markets is almost always structural proximity, not analytical sophistication.

**Gary has argued that the biggest mistake most people make with speculative assets is confusing short-term price action with long-term thesis validity. He has described holding his angel positions and collectible investments through significant drawdowns without losing conviction because he built the thesis on cultural and behavioral observations rather than price charts. Price action is noise; behavioral trajectory is signal.** ([source](Tony Robbins, Unshakeable, Gratitude & Focusing on Your Steak | #AskGaryVee 242))

**Implication:** When you build an investment thesis on observed human behavior rather than financial modeling, drawdowns feel different — they're buying opportunities rather than thesis invalidations. Do the behavioral work first, before prices enter the picture.

**Gary has consistently described his angel investment thesis as buying into people first, markets second.** He made early bets on founders like Evan Spiegel and Travis Kalanick not because the spreadsheet was compelling but because he read the founder's conviction and the cultural moment simultaneously. He has said his due diligence process is more ethnographic than financial — he studies behavior, passion, and timing. ([source](Recode Media Podcast: Entrepreneurship & Startups))

**Implication:** The most reliable early-stage signal is founder conviction plus cultural timing, not financial projection. Build a habit of studying how people actually behave in a market before analyzing what the numbers say the market should do.

**Gary has consistently made the case that art — like sports cards and wine — is underrepresented in most people's investment thinking because it requires cultural literacy that cannot be easily taught in a finance program. He has purchased art for both personal enjoyment and as a long-term store of value, and he argues that the collectors who win over decades are the ones who genuinely love the work rather than treating it purely as an asset allocation decision.** ([source](Gary Vaynerchuk at the Web Summit))

**Implication:** If you want to invest in cultural assets, develop genuine taste first. The people who win in art, wine, and collectibles over the long arc are those for whom the joy of ownership is non-negotiable — they hold when others panic-sell.

**Gary's position on speculative assets has always included a brutal acknowledgment that most individual bets will be wrong. He does not frame his investing track record as a predictive genius story — he frames it as a volume and conviction story. He made enough bets, with deep enough conviction in each, that the winners compounded dramatically while the losers were bounded. Bat rate matters less than magnitude of wins when conviction is genuinely calibrated.** ([source](Recode Media Podcast: Entrepreneurship & Startups))

**Implication:** In speculative investing, the goal isn't to be right more often than you're wrong — it's to be deeply right on the bets where you have genuine information edge. Size those appropriately and accept that a high miss rate is structurally normal.

**Gary has been candid that not all of his speculative bets have worked — he has described positions that went to zero and collectible categories that didn't develop the way he anticipated. His public posture on these losses is notable for its absence of defensiveness or revisionism: he treats failed bets as tuition, recalibrates the thesis if the underlying behavioral observation was wrong, and moves forward without emotional baggage. Loss acceptance is a feature of his investing process, not a bug.** ([source](Gary Vaynerchuk - Bullshit Entrepreneurs - SXSW 2016))

**Implication:** Build an explicit after-action review process for investments that don't work. The question isn't 'how do I feel about this?' but 'was the behavioral observation wrong, was the timing wrong, or was the thesis right and the execution wrong?' Those are three very different learnings.

**Gary's entry into wine was never purely financial — it was rooted in genuine personal passion and deep domain expertise built over years of obsessive study. He has consistently emphasized that investing in things you personally love and understand creates a structural advantage: you see nuance the market misses, you hold through volatility more easily, and you are less likely to be fooled by hype. Passion is not separate from investment thesis — it is the thesis.** ([source](Wine Library TV origin; Marie Forleo and Gary Vaynerchuk Interview))

**Implication:** When evaluating speculative bets, ask whether you love the domain enough to study it obsessively without being paid to. That depth of genuine interest is where informational edges actually live.

**Gary's wine expertise wasn't merely a hobby — it was the original proof of concept for his entire 'passion plus platform equals compounding advantage' theory. He studied wine with such obsessive depth at Wine Library that by the time he launched Wine Library TV, his product knowledge was genuinely differentiated from anything else in the market. That domain depth, built before there was any financial incentive to build it, became the foundation of a $60M business and a public platform.** ([source](Marie Forleo and Gary Vaynerchuk Interview))

**Implication:** The investment that almost always gets ignored is the one you make in deep expertise before there's a clear monetization path. Study something you love beyond what any job requires, and the asymmetric returns eventually appear.

**Gary's approach to collecting has always been explicitly dual-purpose.** he buys things he would be genuinely happy to own forever and simultaneously believes will appreciate. He refuses to buy assets he finds culturally or personally meaningless purely for financial return. This constraint, which might seem to limit his investable universe, actually protects him from getting caught in pure speculative bubbles where there is no underlying cultural passion to sustain demand. ([source](Marie Forleo and Gary Vaynerchuk Interview))

**Implication:** Set a personal rule that you will only invest in collectibles and cultural assets you would genuinely want to own even if they never appreciated. That filter will systematically keep you away from pure speculation without cultural foundation.

**Marketplace dynamics — the supply-and-demand mechanics of platforms like Uber and Airbnb — represent a category of business that Gary is deeply focused on through his investing activity. He sees these businesses as early pioneers in a long wave of marketplace disruption.** ([source](youtube:SXSW_GaryV_Interactive2014))

**Implication:** For investors and founders, marketplace businesses that create genuine supply-demand efficiency represent one of the most durable structural opportunities in tech — the earliest entrants in each vertical capture enormous value.

**Gary has been a prolific angel investor since the early 2010s, with a portfolio that includes Twitter, Tumblr, Snapchat, Uber, Venmo, and Coinbase — all pre-IPO bets made when the companies were still highly speculative. His angel strategy was not to diversify broadly in a calculated way but to concentrate on founders and markets where he had cultural intuition that the mainstream hadn't yet developed. He treated early-stage investing as a natural extension of his attention-spotting skill set.** ([source](CNBC: Secret to Success Interview))

**Implication:** Angel investing is most potent when it follows genuine cultural insight rather than financial modeling. The question isn't 'what are the financials?' but 'does this match where human behavior is actually heading?'

**Gary has made the argument publicly that the traditional financial system's dismissal of collectibles as 'non-serious' asset classes is itself an arbitrage opportunity. When credentialed institutions refuse to cover a market, individual investors with cultural fluency have a sustained information advantage. The moment institutional coverage and legitimacy arrive, that advantage is gone. His consistent advice is to move before the Bloomberg terminal covers it.** ([source](CNBC: Secret to Success Interview))

**Implication:** Track what the financial establishment actively dismisses or ignores and take those dismissals seriously as potential early-mover signals. The gap between cultural passion and institutional legitimacy is where individual investors still have a structural edge.

**Vaynerchuk drew a direct line between his childhood background in collectibles — baseball cards, antiques, sports memorabilia — and his early and confident move into NFTs. His family's familiarity with collecting gave him pattern recognition that others in tech lacked. He saw NFTs not as a foreign technology but as a digital evolution of a market he had navigated since adolescence.** ([source](CNBC: NFT Art Auction))

> *"I grew up with collectibles, antiques and sports cards. My mom and dad were into that stuff too."*

**Implication:** Deep domain expertise in analog markets can be a powerful advantage when those markets go digital. Operators with roots in collectibles, physical goods, or fan culture are often better positioned to read NFT and Web3 markets than pure technologists.

**The lowest listed price for a VeeFriends token at the time of the article remained $50,000, establishing a significant floor for the collection's secondary market. The Christie's auction results ranged from $162,500 for 'Diamond Hands' to $412,500 for 'Empathetic Elephant,' demonstrating wide variance in perceived value across individual pieces. This price floor and range dynamic reflects Vaynerchuk's deliberate positioning of VeeFriends as a premium collectible market, not a mass-access one.** ([source](CNBC: NFT Art Auction))

**Implication:** Premium pricing floors in NFT collections signal intentional scarcity and community curation. Creators building token-based communities must decide early whether they are optimizing for breadth of access or depth of committed holders — the two strategies require fundamentally different pricing architectures.

**Gary traces a direct, unbroken line from hustling baseball cards and Garbage Pail Kids in Edison to becoming an angel investor. The skills he developed — identifying value, buying low, selling high, and reading market demand — were the same whether the asset was a trading card or a startup.** ([source](My Central Jersey: Childhood Profile))

**Implication:** Investing and market intuition can be developed through small-scale collecting and trading long before formal capital is involved. Early pattern recognition in niche markets is transferable to high-stakes investing.

---

## Leadership, Culture & People

**Keeping non-performers around — even with good intentions — erodes company culture and costs businesses the marginal differentiation that separates winners from also-rans. In competitive business, culture is built or destroyed in millimeters.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"It erodes your culture when you don't have candor when you keep people around that shouldn't be around... business is millimeters it's inches these little things are the differentiation between having an exit someday or becoming a world-class company."*

**Implication:** Cultural integrity is a compounding asset — small honest decisions accumulate into organizational excellence, while small acts of avoidance compound into cultural decay.

**Gary acknowledges that a key leadership failure of his — holding back candid feedback and then firing people without warning — stemmed from his discomfort with conflict. He could not see or admit this until he had done enough self-work to feel secure enough to be honest about it.** ([source](youtube:on_purpose_podcast_ep19))

> *"My lack of candor along the way is a shortcoming of mine as a manager. In my lack of being interested in conflict I would hold it to myself but then I would pull the rug from underneath you when I would fire you without giving you context along the way."*

**Implication:** Leaders who avoid conflict as a kindness actually create more harm than those who deliver hard feedback early — the discomfort of honest conversations is far less damaging than the shock of sudden termination.

**Gary takes full ownership for every organizational failure — if an employee underperforms, it's Gary's fault for hiring their manager. This radical accountability framework eliminates the organizational blame culture that makes most companies dysfunctional.** ([source](youtube:on_purpose_podcast_ep19))

> *"I met with two employees today I want to blame their managers but I know it's my fault because I hired their managers."*

**Implication:** True leadership accountability runs all the way to the top — a CEO who blames managers for employee failures is abdicating the only accountability that actually produces organizational change.

**Gary gives feedback to his team almost entirely in terms of strengths and potential, not deficiencies.** His instinct with a team member who is ready to level up is to tell them explicitly what he sees in them — positive affirmation of capability as the primary managerial communication tool. ([source](youtube:on_purpose_podcast_ep19))

**Implication:** Strength-based management feedback — explicitly naming what you see someone capable of — is more motivating and actionable than gap-based feedback and creates psychological safety for bigger risks.

**Self-awareness is cultivated by creating conditions where the people around you feel safe telling you the truth — and then publicly rewarding them when they do. The moment one person is visibly rewarded for honest feedback, others begin to feel safe doing the same.** ([source](podcast:aspire:gary-vaynerchuk-truth-humility-kindness))

> *"Putting the people around you in a position to tell you the truth without ramification. Not only thank you in private — when an employee says something to me, in front of everyone."*

**Implication:** Leaders who want honest input must do the work of making honesty visibly safe and visibly rewarded — because no amount of lip service creates the culture that rewards do.

**Gary has spoken about the critical importance of leaders being consistent — that the greatest trust eroder is saying one thing and doing another, repeatedly, at small scale. Teams don't need leaders to be perfect; they need them to be predictable and honest. A leader who admits they were wrong and corrects course builds more credibility than one who maintains a false posture of certainty.** ([source](GaryVee: This is the #1 Reason You're Unhappy! Do This to Overcome Anxiety & Unlock Emotional Peace!))

**Implication:** Prioritize behavioral consistency over impression management. When you make an error in judgment or communication, name it explicitly and quickly — the act of owning it compounds trust faster than the error depletes it.

**Gary has argued that most organizational dysfunction stems from leaders not having hard conversations early enough.** A problem that is addressed at week two is a coaching conversation; the same problem addressed at month eighteen is a termination. The delay — almost always driven by conflict avoidance — is what makes the situation damaging for everyone involved. ([source](Gary Vee & Jay Shetty ON How To Stop Caring What People Think Of You | ON Purpose Podcast Ep.19))

**Implication:** Build a personal practice of addressing performance and behavior gaps within two weeks of first observing them. Early intervention preserves the relationship and the outcome; late intervention usually destroys both.

**Gary has consistently pushed back on the idea that a leader's job is to be liked.** He distinguishes between being loved for doing the right thing over time and being liked in any given moment — they are often mutually exclusive in the short run. Leaders who optimize for approval ratings compromise their decisions and eventually lose the respect of their best people. ([source](Gary Vaynerchuk: Why Truth, Humility, and Kindness Will Become Your Next Superpower))

**Implication:** Separate popularity from effectiveness as a leadership metric. The decisions that feel most uncomfortable in real time are frequently the ones people remember most positively five years later.

**Gary argues that leaders who tolerate underperformance out of misplaced kindness are actually being cruel — they are denying the individual the feedback they need to grow, and they are penalizing the high performers who have to carry the slack. Genuine accountability, in his framework, is an expression of respect and belief in someone's capability, not a punishment.** ([source](SUCCESS Comes Once You FIGURE THIS OUT! | Gary Vee | Top 10 Rules))

**Implication:** Reframe accountability conversations as acts of investment in the other person. The manager who avoids hard conversations isn't being kind — they're being conflict-avoidant at their team's expense.

**Gary has been consistent that the best hiring signal is hunger and self-awareness, not credentials or experience.** He looks for people who understand their own gaps, are honest about them, and are demonstrably motivated to close them. A self-aware, hungry junior hire will outperform a credentialed but complacent senior hire over any meaningful time horizon. ([source](TIM FERRISS, TRIBE OF MENTORS, ADVICE FOR ENTREPRENEURS & INTERNATIONAL STUDENTS | #ASKGARYVEE 271))

**Implication:** Redesign interview processes to surface self-awareness and intrinsic motivation rather than resume pattern-matching. The people who know what they don't know are the ones most capable of learning what the role actually demands.

**Gary has framed the relationship between leaders and their teams as fundamentally requiring the 51/49 principle — giving slightly more than you take, consistently and without expectation of immediate reciprocity. Over a multi-year period, the compound interest of that orientation builds a workforce that is genuinely loyal rather than contingently employed. The leaders who try to extract maximum output while giving minimum investment eventually find themselves managing a hollow organization.** ([source](Tony Robbins & Gary Vee: Creating a Money Machine | Tony Robbins))

**Implication:** Map every major leadership touchpoint — compensation decisions, development investments, recognition practices, flexibility extended — and honestly assess whether you are giving more than you are extracting. The math catches up over time in both directions.

**Gary views HR not as a compliance function but as a core competitive strategy.** The quality of talent an organization attracts, develops, and retains determines its ceiling more than any product decision or marketing investment. Companies that treat HR as a legal shield rather than a growth lever are leaving their most important asset unmanaged. ([source](WEB SUMMIT GARY VAYNERCHUK KEYNOTE | LISBON PORTUGAL 2016))

**Implication:** Founders and executives should give their head of people the same strategic weight as their CFO or CMO. Talent infrastructure built early compounds into an enormous long-term advantage.

**Gary has described meetings as one of the primary ways organizational productivity is destroyed at scale.** His preference is for voice memos, direct messages, and short asynchronous communication over scheduled group meetings. He believes meeting-heavy cultures reward people for being present rather than productive, which over time selects for the wrong kind of employee. ([source](WEB SUMMIT GARY VAYNERCHUK KEYNOTE | LISBON PORTUGAL 2016))

**Implication:** Audit meeting load and replace recurring status meetings with structured async updates. Reserve synchronous time for decisions that genuinely require real-time debate — and be honest about how few decisions actually qualify.

**Gary has spoken at length about how treating employees as full human beings — not as productivity units — is not just a nice cultural posture but a direct driver of business performance. When people feel genuinely seen and cared for by leadership, their discretionary effort and loyalty increase in ways no compensation package alone can replicate. At VaynerMedia, he has pointed to this human-first orientation as central to the culture's ability to attract and retain strong talent in a competitive industry.** ([source](WEB SUMMIT GARY VAYNERCHUK KEYNOTE | LISBON PORTUGAL 2016))

**Implication:** Invest in learning something genuinely personal about every direct report — their fears, their ambitions, what they're proud of outside of work. This knowledge, when acted on authentically, generates a level of commitment that no bonus structure alone can buy.

**Culture is not defined by the perks a company offers — it is defined by who gets promoted and who gets fired.** Gary has consistently argued that the real values of any organization are revealed in its personnel decisions, not its mission statements or office amenities. When a toxic high-performer keeps their job, the entire workforce receives a message about what actually matters. ([source](Gary Vaynerchuk - Bullshit Entrepreneurs - SXSW 2016))

**Implication:** Leaders should audit their last ten promotion and termination decisions and ask whether they collectively signal the culture they claim to want. Personnel decisions are the loudest culture communication any organization makes.

**Gary has been direct that leaders need to take radical personal accountability for the culture they have — not the culture they intended. If a team is passive, political, or low-energy, the leader created the conditions for that. Blaming circumstances, market pressures, or individual bad actors is a way of avoiding the only intervention that actually changes outcomes: the leader changing their own behavior first.** ([source](Gary Vaynerchuk - Bullshit Entrepreneurs - SXSW 2016))

**Implication:** When diagnosing a culture problem, start with a personal audit of leadership behaviors before diagnosing team behaviors. The culture almost always reflects the leader's unexamined patterns — and fixing it starts there.

**Gary has been direct that leaders who hide behind strategy documents and decks are often avoiding the real work, which is human. The hardest part of building a large organization is not the strategy — it is the daily, unglamorous work of having honest conversations, giving clear feedback, making difficult calls on people, and maintaining cultural standards when it would be easier to look away. He calls out the pattern of leaders retreating into analytical work to avoid relational work.** ([source](Gary Vaynerchuk - Bullshit Entrepreneurs - SXSW 2016))

**Implication:** Assess your personal time allocation and honestly quantify how much of it goes to strategic planning versus direct human leadership. The ratio almost always needs to shift toward the human side as organizations scale.

**Gary has described running VaynerMedia — which grew to over 1,500 employees — as the most humbling and educational leadership experience of his career. He learned that managing at scale forced him to confront his own blind spots in ways that running a small team never could. The complexity of a large organization is where leadership theory gets replaced by leadership reality.** ([source](Adweek: VaynerMedia Rapid Ascent))

**Implication:** Entrepreneurs who want to become genuine leaders should seek scale deliberately, because the organizational problems that arise at 50, 200, and 1,000 people are qualitatively different and cannot be theorized in advance.

**Gary has talked about the specific failure mode where founders scale personally but don't scale their communication practices. What worked as leadership communication for a ten-person team — walking the floor, reading the room, having individual conversations — breaks completely at 200 or 500 people. Leaders who don't deliberately redesign how they communicate at each stage of growth become progressively more disconnected from the organization they are supposed to be leading.** ([source](Adweek: VaynerMedia Rapid Ascent))

**Implication:** At each growth inflection point, explicitly re-architect communication channels, rituals, and feedback loops. The communication infrastructure that got you here will not get you there.

**Gary has consistently emphasized that the best leaders are talent scouts first and managers second.** Identifying people who are undervalued — whether junior employees whose potential hasn't been recognized or experienced people who've been mislabeled — and placing them in the right roles is where the highest-leverage leadership work actually happens. Most organizational underperformance is a placement error, not a talent shortage. ([source](Gary Vaynerchuk Tells You How to Rock SXSW (Full Session) | Interactive 2014 | SXSW))

**Implication:** Spend disproportionate leadership time on talent identification and role design rather than performance management of mismatched hires. Getting the right person in the right role eliminates most of the management problems that consume leadership bandwidth.

**Gary has made the point that a company's content and social media presence is a direct reflection of its internal culture — organizations with strong, honest, energetic cultures produce better content naturally because the people inside them are genuinely engaged. Trying to produce compelling brand content on top of a hollow or fearful culture is like trying to decorate a building with a broken foundation. The internal state leaks out.** ([source](Gary Vaynerchuk Tells You How to Rock SXSW (Full Session) | Interactive 2014 | SXSW))

**Implication:** Treat internal culture investment as upstream content strategy. The most authentic thing any brand can communicate is a genuine internal reality — and audiences increasingly have the sensitivity to distinguish between the two.

**Gary has talked openly about the importance of letting employees own their craft without micromanagement, while still maintaining high output expectations. He distinguishes between managing the person and managing the work — the former is often a form of ego, while the latter is genuine leadership. The best leaders he admires hire people smarter than themselves in specific domains and then get out of the way.** ([source](Marie Forleo and Gary Vaynerchuk Interview))

**Implication:** Define clear outcomes and then extend real autonomy to the people executing them. Micromanagement signals either a hiring error or a leadership insecurity — both need to be addressed directly.

**Gary has argued that the obsession with titles and hierarchy in most organizations is a direct drag on speed and agility. When people's sense of identity is tied to their organizational rank, they defend territory instead of solving problems. The best organizations he admires are ones where people care about the work more than their position — and leaders create that environment deliberately by modeling it themselves.** ([source](Gary Vaynerchuk at the Web Summit))

**Implication:** Be deliberate about how title and hierarchy are used as motivational tools. When people are recruited primarily with title inflation, you've set up a culture where status maintenance eventually outweighs output.

**Insecurity, in Vaynerchuk's view, is not just a personal burden — it actively poisons workplace culture.** He has observed talented employees at VaynerMedia whose anxiety and insecurity make them liabilities regardless of their skill level, to the point where termination becomes necessary. ([source](CNBC: Secret to Success Interview))

> *"Insecurity is a killer. It leads to all the bad s--- and so it's the difference between confidence and security."*

**Implication:** Hiring for confidence and psychological security may matter as much as hiring for technical talent. Leaders should build cultures that actively counteract insecurity rather than ignoring it as a personal problem.

**Gary acknowledges that his personal brand creates initial hesitation among experienced industry hires, but he views the interview process and the first six months on the job as the real proof point. He isn't defensive about the skepticism — he treats it as a natural filter and trusts the experience to convert doubters.** ([source](Adweek: VaynerMedia Rapid Ascent))

> *"Some of them are cautious about working for me—usually until they sit down with me and we go through the interview process. But even then, they're still a little cautious because there is a big personality, and I get that. On the flip side, I feel great about how they feel about me and us six months later."*

**Implication:** Leaders with strong personal brands should expect qualified candidates to arrive with skepticism. The answer isn't to soften the brand — it's to make the onboarding and early experience so strong that it resolves the doubt through direct evidence.

---

## NFTs, Web3 & Emerging Technology

**Voice activation, AI, and augmented reality are Gary's predicted next wave of dominant interfaces, specifically because they will be faster than current touchscreen interactions. Whatever interface reduces the friction between human intent and digital action will win — speed of interface is the core competitive variable.** ([source](youtube:JRE-910-GaryVaynerchuk))

> *"Voice when Alexa can order your package for you instead of like whatever's faster... whatever's faster is what we're going to gravitate towards so I think voice activation AI AR those kind of things are really going to start to populate up over the next half decade."*

**Implication:** Bet on the interface that reduces the most friction for the most common human desires. Voice, AI, and AR all converge on this — invest in building presence in these channels before mass adoption normalizes them.

**Gary frames VeeFriends as potentially the first intellectual property to achieve billion-dollar status that was built from NFTs — analogous to Star Wars being built from film or Harry Potter from books. He sees the medium as the origin of the IP, not just the distribution.** ([source](youtube:QuestPodcast_GaryV))

> *"I believe that v friends will have incredible legacy as the first intellectual property that was a billion dollar intellectual property built from nfts the way that star wars was built from film the way that harry potter was built from books."*

**Implication:** Every new medium eventually births its own defining intellectual property. Betting early on a new medium as an origin story for IP — not just a distribution channel — is how generational franchises are built.

**The characters in VeeFriends — Empathy Elephant, Patient Panda, Accountable Ant — are designed to make emotional traits culturally cool and commercially accessible across streetwear, cartoons, and consumer products. The goal is to embed positive psychology into mainstream IP.** ([source](youtube:QuestPodcast_GaryV))

> *"I have a lot of passion for these beautiful human traits like when I tell you my passion to make accountable ant... I want accountable ant to be like fucking hot like whether I'd make a streetwear brand and do like a sunday morning cartoon or whether I do a cereal."*

**Implication:** Emotional intelligence can be packaged and distributed through beloved characters, not just books and lectures. The most scalable way to teach human values may be through IP that people fall in love with before they realize they're learning.

**The internet and blockchain represent a technology shift comparable to the printing press — not just television or the telephone. This is a foundational transformation of how humans interact, transact, and create value, and it will quietly become the invisible infrastructure behind everything.** ([source](podcast:next_with_novo_gary_vee_blockchain))

> *"It's the biggest technology that's ever happened in the history of life, outside of things like fucking fire. And the printing press is the one that I keep going back to. Television, phone. Fine. But this is like printing press 10.0."*

**Implication:** Entrepreneurs and investors who underestimate the blockchain's foundational nature are making the same mistake as those who dismissed the printing press as a niche tool for monks.

**Blockchain will become invisible infrastructure — society won't notice its presence, but it will fundamentally change the world. Like the back panel of a television, it works silently underneath everything, and its impact will only be obvious in retrospect.** ([source](podcast:next_with_novo_gary_vee_blockchain))

> *"Blockchain's underrated. It's gonna have its day. It's starting to show in 15 years. The interesting thing is you won't notice, it'll be the back of the fucking TV. But it's gonna change the world in a really positive way."*

**Implication:** The biggest technology bets are often the ones that disappear into the background — invest in infrastructure plays before they become obvious.

**NFTs solve a fundamental problem that has always existed in digital art.** the inability to prove scarcity and ownership. The blockchain creates the first true mechanism for digital scarcity, which unlocks an entirely new asset class for artists and collectors alike. ([source](podcast:next_with_novo_gary_vee_blockchain))

> *"Satoshi's White Paper was the first digital signature that you could counterfeit. So what that means you can make digital art that no one can counterfeit. And so all of a sudden art gets its value from scarcity. And so the Blockchain allows you to do that."*

**Implication:** Artists who have never been able to monetize digital work now have a legitimate market — the NFT space isn't a fad but the first real infrastructure for digital ownership.

**The 'flex' — the ability to publicly display ownership — is a critical missing piece for NFT mainstream adoption.** High-net-worth collectors buy physical art partly to show it off. The moment NFTs develop a compelling display and social signaling mechanism, mainstream adoption will accelerate dramatically. ([source](podcast:next_with_novo_gary_vee_blockchain))

> *"The reason sports cards are is 'cause you can take a selfie and put it on Instagram. We love to flex. I'm waiting to understand the flexification of the NFT. And as soon as I understand it... I'm curious what it's gonna be. I want to invest in it because it's gonna be the shovels and picks of that world."*

**Implication:** The 'picks and shovels' investment in the NFT space isn't just the tokens themselves — it's the display, showcase, and social signaling infrastructure that will make NFT ownership culturally legible.

**Crypto is deeply progressive in nature — it disintermediates rent-taking institutions like banks, stock markets, and insurance companies in favor of peer-to-peer value transfer. Progressive politicians who oppose crypto are doing so out of education gaps, not ideological alignment.** ([source](podcast:next_with_novo_gary_vee_blockchain))

**Implication:** Crypto advocates should focus education efforts on progressive policymakers, who are natural ideological allies once they understand what peer-to-peer finance actually means for economic equity.

**The NFT market will eventually create live event moments — concerts, sports, cultural events — where limited digital art pieces are sold in real-time, merging experiential scarcity with digital ownership. The Superbowl combined with a commissioned artist creating a one-of-ten digital work is the model Gary sees coming.** ([source](podcast:next_with_novo_gary_vee_blockchain))

> *"We're gonna have my homes and I'm gonna have an artist do a digital painting of woman or a rapper or sing a little song behind him. And then he's gonna be up there. And you're gonna sell that little clip from the Superbowl done by Chase Hall or whoever the artist is. And people will be like, 'Dude, there's only 10 of those?' And they're gonna buy them and they're gonna keep them."*

**Implication:** The highest value NFTs will be tied to live, unrepeatable cultural moments — event organizers, artists, and platforms that figure out the live-NFT drop experience will capture enormous value.

**Commerce is shifting from a visually-driven discovery model to an audio-centric lead generation model.** This is a fundamental channel inversion, not a marginal shift, and brands that don't adapt their identity to work in audio-first environments will be left behind. ([source](youtube:unknown))

**Implication:** Brands built entirely on visual identity — logo, color, photography — are fragile in a voice-first world. Sonic branding, verbal shorthand, and brand recall through language will become primary strategic assets.

**Gary has positioned AI-generated content as a force that will dramatically compress the cost of content production, effectively removing one of the last remaining barriers between a good idea and its execution. He has expressed the view that the strategic variable in a world of cheap AI-generated content shifts from production capability to creative direction and authentic perspective — because the tools will be equally available to everyone, so the differentiator becomes the human judgment guiding them.** ([source](Business Experts Reveal Growth Secrets! - with Gary Vee, Tom Bilyeu and more))

**Implication:** Invest less in production infrastructure and more in developing a distinctive creative point of view. When AI makes production essentially free, the scarce resource becomes originality, taste, and the authentic human signal that audiences can feel the difference between.

**Gary has acknowledged making mistakes in timing and execution within his NFT bets — including being more vocal than he should have been during the peak of the market without sufficiently emphasizing the risk of specific projects. He has used his own experience with VeeFriends and the broader NFT market as a case study in the difference between being correct about a technology's long-term direction and being correct about the near-term price of assets in that technology. The two are separate questions that require separate answers.** ([source](When You Hyper-Focus on the Results You Miss New Opportunities | The Quest Podcast))

**Implication:** Public technology conviction comes with communication responsibility. If you are genuinely right about a 10-year technology shift, you still owe your audience an honest account of near-term volatility risk. Conflating infrastructure conviction with asset-price confidence is a failure of kind candor toward the people listening to you.

**Gary has argued that the metaverse — regardless of which company's specific vision wins — represents an inevitable expansion of the internet into spatial and immersive computing, and that brands and creators who dismiss it as science fiction are making the same mistake that brands made when they dismissed social media as a teenager's distraction. He doesn't position the metaverse as an immediate obligation for marketers, but as something that demands strategic monitoring and small-scale experimentation now rather than a wait-and-see posture.** ([source](Business Experts Reveal Growth Secrets! - with Gary Vee, Tom Bilyeu and more))

**Implication:** Run small metaverse experiments now at low cost, even if the ROI is not yet measurable. The cost of early experimentation is minimal; the cost of arriving late with no institutional knowledge is enormous. The brands that understood Facebook in 2007 didn't wait for proof — they ran experiments and built understanding while the platform was cheap.

**Gary's core thesis on AI has been consistent with his thesis on every prior technology wave.** the question is not whether AI will change things but how fast and who will be positioned to benefit. He has specifically called out AI tools for content creation and marketing as representing the most significant shift in the attention economy since mobile, because they change the supply side of content production fundamentally — making it possible for a single operator to produce content at what was previously a team-scale volume. ([source](Business Experts Reveal Growth Secrets! - with Gary Vee, Tom Bilyeu and more))

**Implication:** Small operators and solo creators gain the most from AI-powered content tools because they close the production gap with large teams the fastest. If you are a small creator or brand, AI tools are not optional — they are the leverage mechanism that makes the volume strategy viable without proportional headcount growth.

**Gary has argued that the biggest mistake brands made with NFTs was approaching them as a marketing campaign with a defined start and end date rather than as infrastructure for a permanent community ownership model. Brands that treated their NFT drop as a campaign looked for ROI on the same timeline they would evaluate a paid media buy — and naturally concluded the experiment had failed. Brands that treated it as community infrastructure were asking a fundamentally different question with a fundamentally different time horizon.** ([source](Business Experts Reveal Growth Secrets! - with Gary Vee, Tom Bilyeu and more))

**Implication:** Before entering Web3, decide which question you are actually asking: campaign ROI or community infrastructure. The evaluation criteria, time horizon, and success metrics are completely different. Confusing the two is why most brand NFT projects produced disappointment on both dimensions.

**Gary launched VeeFriends in 2021 as a direct demonstration of his conviction that NFTs represented a genuine convergence of technology shift and business model shift simultaneously. Rather than simply talking about Web3, he built a project where NFT ownership functioned as access tokens to real-world events and experiences — making the intellectual property and community utility inseparable. He viewed it as the purest expression of what smart contracts could actually do for creators and brands.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** Builders entering Web3 should ask whether their project creates value that only exists because of the technology — not just slap a blockchain on an existing product. The convergence of IP, access, and ownership in a single token is the model worth studying.

**Gary has consistently framed blockchain not primarily as a financial technology but as an infrastructure layer that fundamentally changes what humans can do on the internet — specifically around ownership and provenance. Before blockchain, digital files were infinitely copyable with no verifiable scarcity; after it, digital ownership becomes provable and programmable. He sees this as the equivalent of the shift from physical to digital commerce, except in the opposite direction: from infinite copies back to verifiable originals.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** Stop evaluating blockchain through a purely financial lens. The more durable question is: what behaviors and business models become possible when digital ownership is verifiable? That's where the 10-year opportunities live.

**Gary has argued that most people dismissed NFTs the same way they dismissed social media, email, and the early web — by focusing on the speculative mania rather than the underlying capability. He acknowledged openly that there was significant froth in NFT prices in 2021-2022, but maintained that the noise of speculation should not be confused with the signal of the technology. The pattern of dismissal followed by regret is one Gary has personally witnessed repeat across every major platform shift of the past three decades.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** Train yourself to separate the speculative cycle from the structural shift. Every transformative technology arrives wrapped in a bubble. The bubble pops; the technology often stays. The investors and builders who stayed after the dot-com crash built the internet we live on.

**Gary has repeatedly compared his bet on NFTs and Web3 to his early recognition of Facebook, Twitter, and YouTube as transformative attention platforms — bets most established operators dismissed as niche or irrelevant at the time. His filter in each case was the same: is real human behavior shifting here, or is this just a feature? When he saw communities forming around digital collectibles with genuine emotional attachment, he read that as the behavior signal that preceded platform maturity in every prior cycle.** ([source](When You Hyper-Focus on the Results You Miss New Opportunities | The Quest Podcast))

**Implication:** The leading indicator for a real platform shift is genuine emotional behavior from real humans — not press coverage, not venture capital volume. Find the community that would be devastated if the platform disappeared. That's where the attention is real.

**Gary's original thesis for VeeFriends was that intellectual property built on top of a blockchain community would compound in ways traditional IP could not — because the holders become stakeholders with aligned economic incentives to promote and protect the IP alongside the creator. He saw NFT ownership as converting passive fans into active co-owners of a brand, which is a fundamentally different relationship than a fan buying a t-shirt or a poster.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** Creators and brands should evaluate Web3 not as a new revenue channel but as a mechanism for converting audience into community ownership. The model shifts the marketing burden from the creator to an aligned network of stakeholders who benefit from the IP's success.

**Gary has been explicit that he got into NFTs not primarily as a financial investment but because he believed the smart contract infrastructure would eventually change how every creative and commercial relationship on the internet is structured. He pointed to the ability to encode royalties into the transaction itself — so creators earn on every secondary sale without trusting any intermediary — as a genuinely new capability that had no analog in prior digital commerce.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** For creators, the royalty-in-the-contract mechanic is the most durable part of Web3 to understand. The ability to participate in the upside of secondary markets without relying on platform policy changes the long-term economics of creative work in a structural way.

**Gary has consistently used the 1994 internet analogy to contextualize where blockchain and NFTs sat in their development cycle — arguing that people criticizing blockchain's clunky user experience or speculative excess in 2021 were equivalent to people dismissing the early web because dial-up was slow and most early websites were useless. He was personally shaped by being early on the internet in 1994 and has used that experience as a calibration tool for evaluating all subsequent platform shifts.** ([source]('1994 Changed My Life': Gary Vaynerchuk on AOC and the internet))

**Implication:** Use the earliest internet period as your mental model for evaluating any new infrastructure technology. The correct question in 1994 wasn't 'is this website good?' — it was 'does this infrastructure eventually become unavoidable?' Ask the same question about Web3.

**Gary has argued that the concept of digital scarcity — made possible by NFTs — is more culturally profound than most technologists appreciate, because scarcity has always been a primary driver of human desire and status signaling. Before blockchain, digital goods were infinitely reproducible and therefore culturally weightless as status objects. By making digital items genuinely scarce and verifiably owned, NFTs plugged digital collecting into the same psychological circuitry that has driven physical collecting for centuries.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** The psychology of collecting — scarcity, provenance, community, status — is ancient and durable. NFTs didn't invent that psychology; they applied existing infrastructure to it. Any project that taps these mechanics with genuine community identity will outlast the projects that are purely speculative.

**Gary has openly acknowledged that many individual NFT projects will fail and that a lot of what was being sold during the 2021 peak had no underlying utility or community — meaning the speculation was detached from any real value creation. But he distinguished sharply between bad projects and a bad technology, drawing the parallel to the hundreds of failed dot-com companies that did not invalidate the internet itself. His confidence in the infrastructure remained intact even as he acknowledged most projects would go to zero.** ([source](When You Hyper-Focus on the Results You Miss New Opportunities | The Quest Podcast))

**Implication:** Maintain the discipline to separate asset-level due diligence from infrastructure-level conviction. Most specific bets in any new technology wave will fail. The game is identifying the technology wave correctly and then finding the few projects with genuine utility and community behind them.

**Gary has consistently argued that his primary filter for any new technology is whether it changes human behavior at the consumer level, not whether it is technically sophisticated. He is notably less interested in the engineering architecture of blockchain than in the behavioral fact that people were assigning real emotional and financial value to digital objects — because that behavioral shift, once established, is what creates durable market opportunity regardless of which specific protocol wins.** ([source](When You Hyper-Focus on the Results You Miss New Opportunities | The Quest Podcast))

**Implication:** Evaluate emerging technologies through a behavioral lens first, technical lens second. Ask: are real humans already changing what they do because of this? If yes, the technical winners will emerge and the opportunity will be real. If no one's behavior has changed, the technology is still theoretical regardless of how impressive the engineering is.

**Gary has described the sports card and physical collectibles market as a direct predecessor and parallel to the NFT market — arguing that if you understand why a 1952 Mickey Mantle card holds value, you understand the fundamental mechanics behind why a verifiably scarce digital collectible can hold value. The underlying human drivers — nostalgia, scarcity, community, provenance, status — are identical. The difference is the delivery mechanism, not the psychology.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** Don't try to understand NFT markets in isolation. Study the 150-year history of physical collecting markets — trading cards, stamps, coins, art — to understand the durable mechanics. The technology is new; the human behavior it is plugging into is ancient.

**Gary has made the case that the creator economy's biggest structural problem — platforms owning the relationship between creators and their audiences — gets solved by Web3 infrastructure. When a creator's audience relationship is encoded in tokens rather than stored in a platform's database, the platform can no longer revoke access, change the algorithm, or extract value from the creator-audience relationship without the creator's consent. He sees this as one of the most consequential shifts in the power balance between platforms and creators.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** Creators building on centralized platforms in 2024 are still tenants, not owners. The strategic question is how to progressively move the audience relationship to infrastructure you actually own — whether that is email, token-gated communities, or other direct connection mechanisms that no platform can switch off.

**Gary has described VeeFriends as a 40-year IP bet — not a 2-year NFT project — framing it explicitly in the same category as Disney or Pokémon: character-based IP that compounds in value across multiple generations and media formats. He chose to anchor the IP in blockchain not because it was trendy but because the ownership and royalty mechanics gave the IP a fundamentally different economic structure than traditional licensing, where the creator loses control of upside the moment IP is licensed downstream.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** If you are building character or world-based IP, evaluate blockchain-based ownership structures for what they do to your long-term economics — not just for the initial mint revenue. The structural change is in who captures value when the IP appreciates across secondary markets, licensing deals, and new media formats.

**Gary has taken the position that the token-gated community model — where owning a specific NFT grants access to a community, event series, or set of services — is the most underappreciated business model innovation to come out of Web3, because it aligns the economic incentives of the community members with the value of the community itself. As the community becomes more valuable, the token appreciates, which attracts more committed members, which makes the community more valuable. He sees this flywheel as structurally superior to the ad-supported community model that dominates social platforms.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** Consider whether a token-gated community model fits your business before defaulting to a subscription or ad-supported model. The key question is whether your community's value compounds when members are economically aligned with its success — if yes, the token model creates a self-reinforcing growth dynamic that subscriptions cannot replicate.

**Gary has consistently pushed back against the framing that Web3 and NFTs are primarily financial instruments, arguing that this framing is what attracts the wrong participants and sets up the inevitable disappointment cycle. His position is that the durable use cases for NFTs are in access, membership, provenance, and IP ownership — and that people who entered the market purely for price appreciation were never engaging with what the technology actually does well. The financial returns, in his view, are a byproduct of genuine utility creation, not the primary value proposition.** ([source](When You Hyper-Focus on the Results You Miss New Opportunities | The Quest Podcast))

**Implication:** Design NFT projects around what the token actually does for the holder beyond price appreciation. Utility-first design attracts holders who stay when prices drop; speculation-first design attracts flippers who leave. Community retention is the metric that predicts which projects survive a market correction.

**Gary has framed his personal investment in VeeFriends and Web3 as an act of intellectual consistency — having spent years telling others to bet on emerging platforms early, he felt obligated to make meaningful personal bets himself rather than merely advise others to take risks he was unwilling to take. He has described putting real capital into Web3 as a form of accountability to his own publicly stated philosophy about early platform adoption.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** The credibility of technology conviction is proportional to the skin you have in the game. Advising others to take early technology bets while staying personally safe is a form of intellectual dishonesty. If you genuinely believe in a technology shift, find the version of the bet that is appropriate to your means and make it.

**Gary has pointed to the fact that in 2021, the majority of Fortune 500 companies had no Web3 strategy whatsoever — the same way they had no social media strategy in 2007. He sees this institutional lag as the predictable result of large organizations optimizing for near-term financial performance while new technology categories incubate at the edges. The opportunity for smaller operators and individual builders is always highest during the window when large players are still dismissing the category as irrelevant.** ([source](When You Hyper-Focus on the Results You Miss New Opportunities | The Quest Podcast))

**Implication:** Institutional indifference to a new technology is not evidence the technology is unimportant — it is frequently the opposite. The most durable competitive advantages in any technology shift go to the operators who build genuine capability during the years when the large players are still debating whether the category matters.

**Gary has observed that the emotional reaction people have to technologies like NFTs — dismissal, ridicule, moral outrage about environmental impact — mirrors almost exactly the reactions that greeted social media, smartphones, and the early internet. He is not dismissive of those concerns but uses the emotional intensity of the reaction itself as a signal that the technology is touching something real about how people relate to value, ownership, and identity. Strong emotional reactions, in his framework, are confirmation that the stakes are real.** ([source](The Blockchain Is Changing What Humans Can Do on the Internet | 'Next With Novo' Podcast))

**Implication:** Pay attention to the emotional temperature of the criticism a new technology receives. Indifference means the technology is genuinely irrelevant; intense ridicule or moral panic often means it is disrupting something people care deeply about. The reaction is itself a signal about what is at stake.

**Wearable technology and the integration of devices into daily life is an inevitable technological progression — not a fad. The same people who resist Google Glass are the same personality types who once resisted Twitter, Facebook, and smartphones.** ([source](youtube:SXSW_GaryV_Interactive2014))

> *"I am utterly stunned by the lack of chest moves and seeing where guys I have bad news we're all going to become robots and a lot of people are sitting here and saying no it's the same people that said they're not going to go on Twitter years ago or ever have a Facebook account or ever download Snapchat."*

**Implication:** Pattern recognition across technology adoption cycles reveals that resistance to new platforms is almost always wrong in the long run — early believers capture disproportionate upside.

**Vaynerchuk created the VeeFriends NFT series, positioning himself as an early mover and vocal advocate in the Web3 and non-fungible token space. His entry into NFTs extended his pattern of identifying and committing to emerging digital platforms and asset classes before mainstream adoption.** ([source](Wikipedia: Gary Vaynerchuk))

**Implication:** Credible voices who enter emerging technology spaces early — and bring their existing audience along — gain disproportionate influence as those spaces mature. The risk of being early is real, but the upside of platform-level positioning is asymmetric.

**Gary Vaynerchuk created VeeFriends, an NFT collection of 10,255 character tokens, and auctioned five original hand-drawn artworks at Christie's for over $1.2 million. Unlike most NFTs sold at Christie's, these were the original doodles hand-drawn by Vaynerchuk himself — not just blockchain-minted digital files. This distinction between original physical creative work and its digital counterpart added unique value to the auction.** ([source](CNBC: NFT Art Auction))

> *"these particular works are the original characters that were hand-drawn by Vaynerchuk himself"*

**Implication:** Creators entering Web3 should consider how original physical or analog creative artifacts can layer additional value on top of digital NFT projects — the two don't have to be mutually exclusive.

**The VeeFriends NFT collection is not just a digital art project — each token comes embedded with a smart contract that allows Vaynerchuk to interact directly with token holders. Holders also receive exclusive three-year access to VeeCon, an annual business conference. This utility-first design transforms the NFT from a speculative asset into a membership and relationship vehicle.** ([source](CNBC: NFT Art Auction))

**Implication:** NFT projects with built-in utility — access, community, events — create stickier long-term value than pure collectibles. Builders launching token-based projects should design real-world benefits into the smart contract from day one.

**Vaynerchuk built the VeeFriends collection around 10,255 unique character NFTs, each carrying a smart contract with metadata enabling direct buyer interaction. The scale of 10,255 tokens is large enough to build a sizable community but curated enough to maintain collectible scarcity. The combination of scale, smart contract interactivity, and event access reflects a systems-level approach to community architecture, not just a one-off art drop.** ([source](CNBC: NFT Art Auction))

**Implication:** The most durable NFT projects are designed as community operating systems — with utility, interaction mechanics, and access built into the token infrastructure — rather than standalone art releases. Founders should think of their NFT collection as a membership platform with a creative wrapper.

---

## Gratitude, Empathy & Humanity in Business

**You cannot fully love other people if you merely tolerate yourself.** Genuine self-esteem is not optional — it is foundational to your capacity to show up fully for your family, your work, and the people around you. ([source](youtube:askgaryvee271))

> *"You cannot love other people fully if you just tolerate yourself. And so if not for yourself, for other people, your family, your loved ones, you have to reconcile bits and pieces inside of you."*

**Implication:** Investing in self-acceptance and internal reconciliation is not selfish — it is a prerequisite for being a high-quality partner, parent, leader, and friend.

**People dealing with mental health struggles should look to others who have successfully navigated the same challenges as proof that stability and creative output are not mutually exclusive. Seeing someone else do it is one of the most powerful motivators for compliance and hope.** ([source](Tim Ferriss, Ray Dalio & Gary Vaynerchuk Talk About Mental Health))

> *"When you start to realize that other people can do it right — he's become incredibly creative because he can deliver on that other stuff."*

**Implication:** Modeling and case studies matter enormously in mental health recovery — connecting struggling individuals to success stories from people with the same diagnosis accelerates belief and compliance.

**People who don't struggle with mental health challenges have an obligation to develop tolerance and understanding toward those who do. Neurotypical success should not breed judgment — it should breed empathy for others navigating harder terrain.** ([source](Tim Ferriss, Ray Dalio & Gary Vaynerchuk Talk About Mental Health))

**Implication:** Mental health advocacy isn't just for those struggling — it requires active empathy-building in the broader population to reduce stigma and create environments where people can seek help without shame.

**When you are genuinely optimistic and feeling good, Gary argues you have an active responsibility to communicate that publicly — because the volume and reach of negativity in media and social platforms is so extreme that positive signal is structurally underrepresented and carries outsized impact when amplified.** ([source](youtube:JRE-910-GaryVaynerchuk))

**Implication:** Optimism communicated at scale is a public good with real ROI — both for the audience who receives it and for the personal brand of the person who projects it consistently. Silence in the face of negativity is not neutrality; it is a surrender of positive influence.

**Care is the new currency in business.** A small, thoughtful personal gesture — knowing what a client values and acting on it — can generate more revenue through referrals and loyalty than any formal marketing campaign. ([source](youtube:top-5-books-entrepreneur))

> *"be very close to your customers get to know who they are what they like and use that as an ability to show appreciation for those that are supporting you care is the new currency"*

**Implication:** The ROI on genuine human attention to customers is asymmetric — a single personalized act can produce compounding business returns that outperform transactional approaches.

**Building high self-esteem without creating entitlement or delusion is one of the hardest parenting challenges — and Gary credits his mother entirely for threading that needle. He sees his public work as an attempt to do for the world what his mother did for him.** ([source](youtube:QuestPodcast_GaryV))

> *"How do you build ridiculous self-esteem while not creating entitlement and delusion is fucking hard... my mom did it for me she cooked that meal so I almost feel like I want to do for the world what my mom did for me."*

**Implication:** The most powerful mission statements are rooted in the deepest personal gifts you've received. If someone changed your life in a specific way, the most authentic version of your purpose may be scaling that same transformation for others.

**Gary's empathy for his father's cynicism deepened when he understood it as a rational adaptation to communist scarcity — where theft was survival and the black market was the economy. Cynicism rooted in lived experience is not a character flaw; it's a logical response to environment.** ([source](youtube:QuestPodcast_GaryV))

> *"I used to blame him and then I got older and realized oh no I'm so wrong my dad was born in a country where that was true in a communist country everybody stole everything because the black market was the country."*

**Implication:** Before dismissing someone's worldview as broken, trace it back to the environment that produced it. Empathy becomes dramatically easier — and more accurate — when you understand the context that forged the belief.

**Compassion and sympathy — even for wealthy people who lose money — are not just moral positions but strategic necessities for building a functional, non-polarized society. Celebrating the downfall of people we dislike, regardless of their wealth, feeds the separation that makes collective progress impossible.** ([source](podcast:next_with_novo_gary_vee_blockchain))

> *"I think compassion and sympathy is a required way to get back to a non-separating society."*

**Implication:** Leaders who model genuine empathy across class and ideological lines — including toward 'undeserving' targets — are making a long-term investment in the social fabric that benefits everyone including themselves.

**Gratitude and anger cannot coexist in the same moment — they are biochemically and psychologically mutually exclusive states. This is not a soft spiritual claim but a practical operating principle: deliberately cultivating gratitude functionally eliminates the fear and anger that undermine good decision-making.** ([source](youtube:Tony Robbins & Gary Vee: Creating a Money Machine))

> *"You can't be angry and grateful simultaneously they'll you have to notice the only AB Duke that really works and you can't be fearful and grateful simultaneously."*

**Implication:** Gratitude practice is not just emotional hygiene — it is a cognitive performance tool that directly improves business judgment by eliminating fear-based and anger-driven decisions.

**Priming — deliberately engineering your mental and emotional state at the start of each day through structured breathing, gratitude focus, and visualization — is a systematic alternative to hoping you'll feel motivated. A 10-minute daily priming practice creates a consistent biochemical foundation for peak performance.** ([source](youtube:Tony Robbins & Gary Vee: Creating a Money Machine))

> *"I get up every morning and I make a radical change in my state and I have a simple deal with myself I prime for 10 minutes every day because if you know have 10 minutes you have a life you know excuse."*

**Implication:** Emotional and cognitive state is not something that happens to you — it is something you architect through deliberate morning practice, and the architecture of that state determines the quality of every decision and interaction that follows.

**Gary's gratitude practice uses contrast — briefly imagining the loss of his closest family members — to instantly generate intense appreciation for their presence. This visceral contrast technique produces a deeper and more immediate gratitude response than intellectual affirmation.** ([source](youtube:Tony Robbins & Gary Vee: Creating a Money Machine))

> *"I do something very similar every single day I make pretend that my mother dad sister brother wife or children are killed now I know this is a different version of it I know it's a little different sometimes even in detail but I will tell you it's very fleeting it usually happens but I promise you I know it's a little left-field it's insane what that perspective does for me."*
